<script>
	function goToTopic(topic) {
		var topicMap = applySpecialCharacterMapping(topic); 
		var url = "/xpl/topicbrowse.jsp?topic=" + escape(topicMap);
		window.top.location.href = url;
	}
</script>

<div id="BodyWrapper" class="ArticlePage" xmlns:ieee="http://www.ieeexplore.ieee.org"><div id="article">
<div class="section" id="sec1"><div class="header article-hdr"><div class="kicker">		                        SECTION I.</div><h2>Introduction</h2></div><p>Radio frequency identification (RFID) technology is useful because of its nontouching detection characteristic. For UHF (0.9085–0.914 GHz) RFID antenna research in Korea, one of the design considerations is antenna size reduction. Generally, the input impedance of a reduced size antenna is highly reactive that becomes troublesome in the antenna design. Proper impedance matching techniques are, therefore, needed for miniaturized antenna designs.</p><p>In this letter, two matching techniques have been proposed for UHF RFID loop antennas. One is to generate dual-resonance modes inside a loop antenna. The other is to use a bent folded-dipole antenna with a capacitive loading. These antennas are designed for 50 Ohm input impedance for general applications. Both techniques are explained theoretically and verified through simulations (CST MWS) and fabrication.</p></div>
<div class="section" id="sec2"><div class="header article-hdr"><div class="kicker">		                        SECTION II.</div><h2>Loop Antenna Size Reduction Techniques</h2></div><div class="section_2" id="sec2a"><h3>A. A Reduced Size Loop Antenna Characteristics</h3><p><a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig1" class="fulltext-link">Fig. 1(a)</a> shows the comparison of a large conventional loop antenna and the proposed small size loop antenna. The perimeter of the large loop antenna is greater than one wavelength, and it resonates when the total loop length is half-wavelength or even multiple of the half-wavelength. If the total loop length is about half-wavelength, its input impedance is quite reactive, which means the input impedance point is on the right part of the smith chart as shown in <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig1" class="fulltext-link">Fig. 1(b) and (c)</a> <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref1" id="context_ref_1_2a">[1]</a>. In order to match the input impedance of the loop antenna in the given size (40mm×33mm) as shown in <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig1" class="fulltext-link">Fig. 1(c)</a>, both the input resistance and the input reactance should be appropriately designed.</p><p>One of the possible ways for impedance matching is to increase the electrical length by using a multiturn loop. However, it does not suggest the optimal matching point in the small-sized antenna due to the mutual coupling between multiturn loops. Therefore, more efficient and effective matching techniques are necessary for the small antenna design.</p><div class="figure figure-full" id="fig1"><!--          Workaround for combined images.Eg.- 1000116 Fig. 5        --><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/7727/4808186/4770194/4770194-fig-1-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/7727/4808186/4770194/4770194-fig-1-small.gif" alt="Figure 1" /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">Fig. 1. </b><fig><p>(a) Loop antenna size comparison. (b) The proposed loop antenna size <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview"></span><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-1-Frame" tabindex="0"><nobr><span class="math" id="MathJax-Span-1" role="math" style="width: 7.08em; display: inline-block;"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 5.625em; height: 0px; font-size: 126%;"><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(1.326em 1005.56em 2.715em -999.997em); top: -2.245em; left: 0.003em;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-2"><span class="mo" id="MathJax-Span-3" style="font-family: MathJax_Main;">(</span><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-4" style="font-family: MathJax_Math-italic;">λ</span><span class="mo" id="MathJax-Span-5" style="font-family: MathJax_Main; padding-left: 0.268em;">=</span><span class="mn" id="MathJax-Span-6" style="font-family: MathJax_Main; padding-left: 0.268em;">330</span><span class="texatom" id="MathJax-Span-7"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-8"><span class="mstyle" id="MathJax-Span-9"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-10"><span class="mtext" id="MathJax-Span-11" style="font-family: MathJax_Main;">mm</span></span></span></span></span><span class="mo" id="MathJax-Span-12" style="font-family: MathJax_Main;">)</span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 2.252em;"></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; vertical-align: -0.413em; border-left: 0px solid; width: 0px; height: 1.421em;"></span></span></nobr></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-1">(\lambda = 330 {\hbox{mm}})</script></tex-math></inline-formula>. (c) The impedance locus of the proposed loop antenna in the smith chart.</p></fig></div><p class="links"><a href="/document/4770194/all-figures" class="all">View All</a></p></div><p></p></div><div class="section_2" id="sec2b"><h3>B. Type-1 (Meander-Line Loop Dipole Composite Antenna)</h3><p>The matching technique of the Type-1 is using dual-resonance mode inside a loop antenna shown in <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig1" class="fulltext-link">Fig. 1(b)</a>. For small antenna applications, an appropriate loading inside an antenna can lead to size reduction and proper matching. Typically, the capacitive loading or inductive loading are common techniques, which eventually increase electrical length of an antenna, to make an antenna physically small <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref2" id="context_ref_2_2b">[2]</a>, <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref3" id="context_ref_3_2b">[3]</a>. However, when both loadings are simultaneously applied inside an antenna, a resonance characteristic that results in antenna size reduction can be obtained.</p><p>The Type-1 loop antenna consists of capacitive loading and inductive loading which act as an LC tank circuit for the first resonance mode. <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig2" class="fulltext-link">Fig. 2(a)</a> describes the conceptual equivalent circuit model. The total length between point A and point B is less than one wavelength. All about equivalent circuit model and details for the resonance frequency are described in the <a ref-type="app" anchor="app1" class="fulltext-link">Appendix</a>. From the <a ref-type="app" anchor="app1" class="fulltext-link">Appendix</a>, the resonant operating frequency can be approximately calculated by numerically solving <a ref-type="disp-formula" anchor="deqn8" href="#deqn8" class="fulltext-link">(8)</a>, when a parasitic patch is detached in the bottom layer. A parasitic patch has effect on lowering its resonance frequency, and can be used for retuning frequency. However, the second resonance mode is required for the increase of the input resistance. With only one resonance mode, the required bandwidth can be hardly obtained. The second resonance mode is obtained through a dipole antenna in <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig2" class="fulltext-link">Fig. 2(b)</a> which is approximately half-wavelength inside a loop antenna. <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig2" class="fulltext-link">Fig. 2(c) and (d)</a> show the matched antenna and the input impedance locus in the smith chart. In the smith chart, the locus rounds twice due to its dual resonance frequencies, which eventually increase the bandwidth. The locus is marked from 0.8 to 1.0 GHz in the simulation, and it goes through the center of the smith chart, where voltage standing wave ratio (VSWR) is less than 2. The simulation is carried out in the given substrate features: thickness 0.787 mm and dielectric constant <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview"></span><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-2-Frame" tabindex="0"><nobr><span class="math" id="MathJax-Span-13" role="math" style="width: 4.267em; display: inline-block;"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 3.847em; height: 0px; font-size: 111%;"><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(1.384em 1003.79em 2.586em -999.997em); top: -2.219em; left: 0.003em;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-14"><span class="msubsup" id="MathJax-Span-15"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 1.204em; height: 0px;"><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(3.366em 1000.42em 4.207em -999.997em); top: -4.021em; left: 0.003em;"><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-16" style="font-family: MathJax_Math-italic;">ε</span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 4.027em;"></span></span><span style="position: absolute; top: -3.901em; left: 0.483em;"><span class="texatom" id="MathJax-Span-17"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-18"><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-19" style="font-size: 70.7%; font-family: MathJax_Math-italic;">r</span><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-20" style="font-size: 70.7%; font-family: MathJax_Math-italic;">e</span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 4.027em;"></span></span></span></span><span class="mo" id="MathJax-Span-21" style="font-family: MathJax_Main; padding-left: 0.303em;">=</span><span class="mn" id="MathJax-Span-22" style="font-family: MathJax_Main; padding-left: 0.303em;">2.2</span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 2.225em;"></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; vertical-align: -0.263em; border-left: 0px solid; width: 0px; height: 1.07em;"></span></span></nobr></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-2">\varepsilon _{re} = 2.2</script></tex-math></inline-formula>. <a ref-type="table" anchor="table1" class="fulltext-link">Table I</a> shows design parameters for the Type-1 antenna.</p><div class="figure figure-full" id="fig2"><!--          Workaround for combined images.Eg.- 1000116 Fig. 5        --><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/7727/4808186/4770194/4770194-fig-2-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/7727/4808186/4770194/4770194-fig-2-small.gif" alt="Figure 2" /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">Fig. 2. </b><fig><p>(a) Equivalent models of capacitive and inductive loadings. (b) Bent dipole antenna. (c) Composite structure inside the loop antenna. (d) Impedance locus in the smith chart.</p></fig></div><p class="links"><a href="/document/4770194/all-figures" class="all">View All</a></p></div><div class="figure figure-full table" id="table1"><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">Table I </b>Parameters for the Type-1 Antenna [Millimeter]<div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></div><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/7727/4808186/4770194/4770194-table-1-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/7727/4808186/4770194/4770194-table-1-small.gif" alt="Table I" /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div></div><p></p></div><div class="section_2" id="sec2c"><h3>C. Type-2 (Folded Dipole Loop Antenna)</h3><p>The other matching technique concept is shown in <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig3" class="fulltext-link">Fig. 3(a) and (b)</a>. In the given size (40 mm ×33 mm), the total folded dipole loop length is about half-wavelength which is the same length for resonance condition of the dipole antenna. One of the advantages of the half-wavelength folded dipole antenna is that the input resistance can be increased by the parasitic element with the impedance step-up ratio <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview"></span><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-3-Frame" tabindex="0"><nobr><span class="math" id="MathJax-Span-23" role="math" style="width: 4.027em; width: 0px; height: 4.027em;"></span></span></span></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 4.027em;"></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; vertical-align: -0.397em; border-left: 0px solid; width: 0px; height: 1.403em;"></span></span></nobr></span></div><script type="math/tex; mode=display" id="MathJax-Element-4">Z_{IN} = (1 + \alpha)^{2} Z_{D}.\eqno{\hbox{(1)}}</script></tex-math><span class="formula"><span class="link">View Source</span><img aria-describedby="qtip-0" style="display:inline;" title="Right-click on figure or equation for MathML and additional features." data-hasqtip="0" class="qtooltip moreInfo" alt="Right-click on figure for MathML and additional features." src="/assets/img/icon.support.gif" border="0" height="20" width="24" /><span class="tex tex2jax_ignore" style="display:none;">Z_{IN} = (1 + \alpha)^{2} Z_{D}.\eqno{\hbox{(1)}}</span></span></disp-formula>The <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview"></span><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-5-Frame" tabindex="0"><nobr><span class="math" id="MathJax-Span-70" role="math" style="width: 1.505em; display: inline-block;"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 1.324em; height: 0px; font-size: 111%;"><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(1.324em 1001.32em 2.465em -999.997em); top: -2.159em; left: 0.003em;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-71"><span class="msubsup" id="MathJax-Span-72"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 1.324em; height: 0px;"><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(3.186em 1000.72em 4.207em -999.997em); top: -4.021em; left: 0.003em;"><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-73" style="font-family: MathJax_Math-italic;">Z<span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; height: 1px; width: 0.063em;"></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 4.027em;"></span></span><span style="position: absolute; top: -3.901em; left: 0.664em;"><span class="texatom" id="MathJax-Span-74"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-75"><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-76" style="font-size: 70.7%; font-family: MathJax_Math-italic;">D</span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 4.027em;"></span></span></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 2.165em;"></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; vertical-align: -0.197em; border-left: 0px solid; width: 0px; height: 1.07em;"></span></span></nobr></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-5">Z_{D}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> is the input impedance of the single dipole antenna <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref1" id="context_ref_1_2c">[1]</a>, <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref4" id="context_ref_4_2c">[4]</a>. The term <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview"></span><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-6-Frame" tabindex="0"><nobr><span class="math" id="MathJax-Span-77" role="math" style="width: 0.724em; display: inline-block;"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 0.664em; height: 0px; font-size: 111%;"><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(1.565em 1000.6em 2.345em -999.997em); top: -2.159em; left: 0.003em;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-78"><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-79" style="font-family: MathJax_Math-italic;">α</span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 2.165em;"></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; vertical-align: -0.063em; border-left: 0px solid; width: 0px; height: 0.67em;"></span></span></nobr></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-6">\alpha</script></tex-math></inline-formula> depends on the widths of the driven element and parasitic element or the spacing between these two elements. If the widths and spacing are adjusted, the input resistance can be increased for the matched condition. For fine frequency tuning, a capacitive loading is added inside the folded dipole loop antenna shown in <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig3" class="fulltext-link">Fig. 3(b)</a>. A parasitic patch in the bottom layer is applied to increase the loading effect, and its effect lowers resonance frequency. In <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig3" class="fulltext-link">Fig. 3(c)</a>, the smith chart locus follows 50ohm line, and it resonates at 0.912 GHz in the simulation result. Its impedance locus passes through a region VSWR circles like the Type-1 antenna does. The simulation is carried out by using the same substrate, and <a ref-type="table" anchor="table2" class="fulltext-link">Table II</a> shows design parameters for the Type-2 antenna.</p><div class="figure figure-full" id="fig3"><!--          Workaround for combined images.Eg.- 1000116 Fig. 5        --><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/7727/4808186/4770194/4770194-fig-3-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/7727/4808186/4770194/4770194-fig-3-small.gif" alt="Figure 3" /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">Fig. 3. </b><fig><p>(a) Folded dipole loop antenna. (b) Folded dipole loop with capacitive loading. (c) Impedance locus in the smith chart.</p></fig></div><p class="links"><a href="/document/4770194/all-figures" class="all">View All</a></p></div><div class="figure figure-full table" id="table2"><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">Table II </b>Parameters for the Type-2 Antenna [millimeter]<div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></div><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/7727/4808186/4770194/4770194-table-2-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/7727/4808186/4770194/4770194-table-2-small.gif" alt="Table II" /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div></div><div class="figure figure-full table" id="table3"><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">Table III </b>Bandwidths and Maximum Gains<div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></div><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/7727/4808186/4770194/4770194-table-3-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/7727/4808186/4770194/4770194-table-3-small.gif" alt="Table III" /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div></div><p></p></div></div>
<div class="section" id="sec3"><div class="header article-hdr"><div class="kicker">		                        SECTION III.</div><h2>Results</h2></div><p><a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig4" class="fulltext-link">Fig. 4(a) and (b)</a> show the fabricated antennas. For the Type-1 antenna, the parameters in <a ref-type="table" anchor="table1" class="fulltext-link">Table I</a> give the optimized bandwidth. However, its measurement data was slightly different from the simulation results due to complex structure inside loop. Between the simulation and measurement based on <a ref-type="table" anchor="table1" class="fulltext-link">Table I</a>, around 2% resonance frequency shifting is observed. For the Type-2 antenna, simulation and measurement results agree well each other, and its gain and bandwidth characteristics have shown better performance than those of the Type-1 antenna. <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig5" class="fulltext-link">Fig. 5(a) – (c)</a> shows the measured radiation patterns of the proposed antennas. The peak gains and bandwidths for both antennas are listed in the <a ref-type="table" anchor="table3" class="fulltext-link">Table III</a>. The simulated radiation efficiencies of the Type-1 and the Type-2 are 0.42 and 0.93, respectively. In case of the Type-1 antenna, the loading effects can cause low radiation efficiency due to ohmic loss in the meander lines. Bandwidths of both the antennas are equal or greater than 6 MHz, which satisfy UHF RFID band in Korea.</p><div class="figure figure-full" id="fig4"><!--          Workaround for combined images.Eg.- 1000116 Fig. 5        --><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/7727/4808186/4770194/4770194-fig-4-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/7727/4808186/4770194/4770194-fig-4-small.gif" alt="Figure 4" /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">Fig. 4. </b><fig><p>Fabricated antennas: (a) Type-1. (b) Type-2. (c) Return losses.</p></fig></div><p class="links"><a href="/document/4770194/all-figures" class="all">View All</a></p></div><div class="figure figure-full" id="fig5"><!--          Workaround for combined images.Eg.- 1000116 Fig. 5        --><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/7727/4808186/4770194/4770194-fig-5-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/7727/4808186/4770194/4770194-fig-5-small.gif" alt="Figure 5" /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">Fig. 5. </b><fig><p>Measured radiation patterns: (a) <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview"></span><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-7-Frame" tabindex="0"><nobr><span class="math" id="MathJax-Span-80" role="math" style="width: 1.326em; display: inline-block;"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 1.062em; height: 0px; font-size: 126%;"><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(1.524em 1001.06em 2.583em -999.997em); top: -2.179em; left: 0.003em;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-81"><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-82" style="font-family: MathJax_Math-italic;">x</span><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-83" style="font-family: MathJax_Math-italic;">y<span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; height: 1px; width: 0.003em;"></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 2.186em;"></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; vertical-align: -0.329em; border-left: 0px solid; width: 0px; height: 1.004em;"></span></span></nobr></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-7">xy</script></tex-math></inline-formula>-plane. (b) <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview"></span><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-8-Frame" tabindex="0"><nobr><span class="math" id="MathJax-Span-84" role="math" style="width: 1.194em; display: inline-block;"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 0.929em; height: 0px; font-size: 126%;"><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(1.524em 1000.93em 2.583em -999.997em); top: -2.179em; left: 0.003em;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-85"><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-86" style="font-family: MathJax_Math-italic;">y<span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; height: 1px; width: 0.003em;"></span></span><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-87" style="font-family: MathJax_Math-italic;">z<span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; height: 1px; width: 0.003em;"></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 2.186em;"></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; vertical-align: -0.329em; border-left: 0px solid; width: 0px; height: 1.004em;"></span></span></nobr></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-8">yz</script></tex-math></inline-formula>-plane. (c) <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview"></span><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-9-Frame" tabindex="0"><nobr><span class="math" id="MathJax-Span-88" role="math" style="width: 1.26em; display: inline-block;"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 0.995em; height: 0px; font-size: 126%;"><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(1.524em 1000.93em 2.384em -999.997em); top: -2.179em; left: 0.003em;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-89"><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-90" style="font-family: MathJax_Math-italic;">z<span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; height: 1px; width: 0.003em;"></span></span><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-91" style="font-family: MathJax_Math-italic;">x</span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 2.186em;"></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; vertical-align: -0.079em; border-left: 0px solid; width: 0px; height: 0.754em;"></span></span></nobr></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-9">zx</script></tex-math></inline-formula>-plane.</p></fig></div><p class="links"><a href="/document/4770194/all-figures" class="all">View All</a></p></div><p></p></div>
<div class="section" id="sec4"><div class="header article-hdr"><div class="kicker">		                        SECTION IV.</div><h2>Conclusion</h2></div><p>Two matching techniques are proposed for general applications of UHF RFID antenna in Korea. For the Type-1 antenna, dual resonance modes are created inside loop antenna. The first resonance mode is from the inductive and capacitive loadings, and the second resonance mode is from a dipole antenna inside the loop for matching condition. For the Type-2 antenna, the folded dipole loop is formed with the capacitive loading. The folded dipole increases the input resistance, and the capacitive loading lowers the resonant frequency. Both the proposed techniques are suitable for matching small loop antennas, and realized 80% size reduction compared with a conventional one-wavelength loop antenna.</p><div class="figure figure-full" id="fig6"><!--          Workaround for combined images.Eg.- 1000116 Fig. 5        --><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/7727/4808186/4770194/4770194-fig-6-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/7727/4808186/4770194/4770194-fig-6-small.gif" alt="Figure 6" /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">Fig. 6. </b><fig><p>(a) Coplanar strip line. (b) Capacitive loading model. (c) Inductive loading model. (c) Combined structure of capacitive and inductive loading.</p></fig></div><p class="links"><a href="/document/4770194/all-figures" class="all">View All</a></p></div><p></p></div>

<script>

function getCookie(name) {
	var nameEQ = name + "=";
	var ca = document.cookie.split(';');
	for(var i=0;i &lt; ca.length;i++) {
		var c = ca[i];
		while (c.charAt(0)==' ') c = c.substring(1,c.length);
		if (c.indexOf(nameEQ) == 0) return c.substring(nameEQ.length,c.length);
	}
	return null;
}

	{
		if(IBP_WS_ENABLED_FLAG){
		Modal.refreshLegacyAccountTransition('/xpl/mwLegacyAccountTransition.jsp');
		}
	}
});

</script>
